## Chapter 7: Behavior Properties / Multi-vehicle Operations:




### Subsection: 7.1a Introduction to Multi-vehicle Operations

Multi-vehicle operations in marine autonomy refer to the coordination and control of multiple autonomous vehicles in a marine environment. This is a complex and challenging task, as it requires the integration of various technologies and systems. In this section, we will provide an overview of multi-vehicle operations and discuss the key principles and technologies involved.

#### Principles of Multi-vehicle Operations

The principles of multi-vehicle operations are based on the concept of distributed control, where each vehicle is equipped with its own control system and communicates with other vehicles to coordinate their actions. This approach allows for scalability and flexibility, as the number of vehicles can be easily increased or decreased without the need for a centralized control system.

One of the key principles of multi-vehicle operations is the use of advanced sensors and communication systems. These systems are essential for enabling vehicles to perceive their environment, communicate with other vehicles, and make decisions based on this information. Some of the commonly used sensors in marine autonomy include cameras, radar, sonar, and acoustic sensors.

#### Technologies for Multi-vehicle Operations

The development of multi-vehicle operations has been driven by advancements in technology, particularly in the areas of artificial intelligence, machine learning, and communication protocols. These technologies have enabled the development of sophisticated control algorithms and communication systems that are essential for coordinating the actions of multiple vehicles.

One of the key technologies for multi-vehicle operations is the use of artificial intelligence (AI) and machine learning (ML) algorithms. These algorithms are used to make decisions based on the information received from sensors and communicate with other vehicles to coordinate their actions. This allows for more efficient and effective coordination of multiple vehicles.

Another important technology for multi-vehicle operations is the use of communication protocols. These protocols define the rules and procedures for communication between vehicles, allowing them to exchange information and coordinate their actions. Some commonly used communication protocols in marine autonomy include Wi-Fi, Bluetooth, and acoustic modems.

#### Challenges and Considerations

Despite the advancements in technology, there are still several challenges and considerations that need to be addressed in multi-vehicle operations. One of the main challenges is the development of robust and reliable communication systems. As vehicles operate in a marine environment, they may experience interference from other sources, making it difficult to establish a reliable communication link.

Another challenge is the integration of different technologies and systems. As multi-vehicle operations involve the use of various sensors, communication systems, and control algorithms, it is crucial to ensure that they are compatible and can work together seamlessly. This requires extensive testing and validation, which can be time-consuming and costly.

In addition, there are also ethical and societal considerations that need to be addressed in multi-vehicle operations. As these operations involve the use of autonomous vehicles, there are concerns about safety, privacy, and the impact on the marine environment. It is essential to address these concerns and develop guidelines and regulations to ensure responsible and ethical use of marine autonomy.

In conclusion, multi-vehicle operations in marine autonomy are a complex and challenging task, but with the advancements in technology and the development of robust communication systems, it is becoming a reality. By understanding the principles and technologies involved, as well as addressing the challenges and considerations, we can pave the way for a more efficient and sustainable future for marine operations.





### Section: 7.1b Basic Multi-vehicle Operations

In this section, we will discuss the basic principles and techniques used in multi-vehicle operations. These include the use of distributed control, advanced sensors and communication systems, and artificial intelligence and machine learning algorithms.

#### Distributed Control

Distributed control is a key principle in multi-vehicle operations. It allows for the coordination of multiple vehicles without the need for a centralized control system. Each vehicle is equipped with its own control system and communicates with other vehicles to coordinate their actions. This approach is scalable and flexible, as the number of vehicles can be easily increased or decreased without the need for a centralized control system.

#### Advanced Sensors and Communication Systems

Advanced sensors and communication systems are essential for enabling vehicles to perceive their environment, communicate with other vehicles, and make decisions based on this information. Some of the commonly used sensors in marine autonomy include cameras, radar, sonar, and acoustic sensors. These sensors provide information about the vehicle's surroundings, allowing it to make decisions and coordinate its actions with other vehicles.

#### Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning Algorithms

Artificial intelligence (AI) and machine learning (ML) algorithms are used to make decisions based on the information received from sensors and communicate with other vehicles to coordinate their actions. These algorithms are constantly learning and adapting, allowing for more efficient and effective coordination of multiple vehicles.

#### Multi-vehicle Operations in Marine Autonomy

Multi-vehicle operations in marine autonomy have a wide range of applications, including search and rescue operations, environmental monitoring, and underwater exploration. These operations require the coordination of multiple vehicles to achieve a common goal, and the use of distributed control, advanced sensors and communication systems, and AI and ML algorithms is crucial for their success.

In the next section, we will discuss some specific examples of multi-vehicle operations in marine autonomy, including the use of unmanned surface vehicles (USVs) and autonomous underwater vehicles (AUVs) for environmental monitoring and underwater exploration.





### Section: 7.1c Advanced Multi-vehicle Operations

In this section, we will delve deeper into advanced multi-vehicle operations, discussing more complex techniques and applications. These include the use of swarm intelligence, advanced communication protocols, and the integration of artificial intelligence and machine learning algorithms.

#### Swarm Intelligence

Swarm intelligence is a concept inspired by the behavior of social insects, such as ants and bees, where individual agents work together to achieve a common goal. In the context of marine autonomy, swarm intelligence can be used to coordinate multiple vehicles to perform complex tasks, such as mapping large areas or searching for a specific target. Each vehicle acts as an individual agent, communicating with other agents to make decisions and coordinate their actions.

#### Advanced Communication Protocols

Advanced communication protocols are essential for enabling efficient and reliable communication between multiple vehicles. These protocols can include techniques such as time-division multiple access (TDMA), frequency-division multiple access (FDMA), and code-division multiple access (CDMA). These protocols allow for the efficient use of limited communication resources and ensure reliable communication between vehicles.

#### Integration of Artificial Intelligence and Machine Learning Algorithms

The integration of artificial intelligence (AI) and machine learning (ML) algorithms is crucial for enabling advanced multi-vehicle operations. These algorithms can be used to make decisions based on the information received from sensors and communicate with other vehicles to coordinate their actions. They can also learn from past experiences and adapt to changing environments, making them essential for complex multi-vehicle operations.

#### Applications of Advanced Multi-vehicle Operations

Advanced multi-vehicle operations have a wide range of applications in marine autonomy. These include search and rescue operations, environmental monitoring, underwater exploration, and military operations. The use of advanced techniques, such as swarm intelligence and advanced communication protocols, allows for more efficient and effective coordination of multiple vehicles, leading to improved performance and mission success.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the behavior properties and multi-vehicle operations in marine autonomy. We have discussed the principles of distributed control, advanced sensors and communication systems, and the use of artificial intelligence and machine learning algorithms. We have also delved into more advanced techniques, such as swarm intelligence and advanced communication protocols, and their applications in marine autonomy. As technology continues to advance, these techniques will become even more crucial for enabling efficient and effective multi-vehicle operations in the marine environment.

We will begin by discussing the concept of marine autonomy and its relevance to multi-vehicle operations. Marine autonomy refers to the ability of a vessel or system to operate independently, without human intervention. This includes the use of advanced sensors, communication systems, and control algorithms to enable autonomous decision-making and navigation. We will explore the various technologies and techniques used in marine autonomy, and how they contribute to multi-vehicle operations.

Next, we will delve into the topic of sensing in marine autonomy. Sensing plays a crucial role in multi-vehicle operations, as it allows for the detection and tracking of other vehicles, as well as the monitoring of environmental conditions. ### Introduction

In the previous chapters, we have explored the fundamentals of marine autonomy, sensing, and communications. We have discussed the basic principles, technologies, and applications of these concepts. However, as the field of marine autonomy continues to advance, there is a growing need for more complex and sophisticated systems. This is where the concept of nested autonomy comes into play.

The nested autonomy paradigm is a novel approach to designing and implementing marine autonomous systems. It is based on the idea of creating a hierarchy of autonomous agents, each with its own set of tasks and responsibilities. This allows for a more efficient and effective management of complex systems, as well as the ability to handle dynamic and uncertain environments.

In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the concept of nested autonomy and explore its various aspects. We will discuss the principles behind nested autonomy, its advantages and limitations, and its applications in marine autonomy. We will also examine the challenges and future directions of this paradigm.

As we continue to push the boundaries of marine autonomy, the nested autonomy paradigm will play a crucial role in shaping the future of this field. It will enable us to create more advanced and capable autonomous systems, paving the way for a more sustainable and efficient use of our oceans. So let us embark on this journey together and explore the exciting world of nested autonomy in marine autonomy.




### Subsection: 8.1a Introduction to Nested Autonomy Paradigm

The nested autonomy paradigm is a revolutionary approach to designing and implementing marine autonomous systems. It is based on the concept of creating a hierarchy of autonomous agents, each with its own set of tasks and responsibilities. This allows for a more efficient and effective management of complex systems, as well as the ability to handle dynamic and uncertain environments.

The nested autonomy paradigm is a natural extension of the traditional single-vehicle autonomy approach. In single-vehicle autonomy, a single agent is responsible for all tasks and decisions. However, as systems become more complex and dynamic, this approach becomes increasingly difficult to manage. The nested autonomy paradigm addresses this issue by breaking down the system into smaller, more manageable subsystems, each with its own autonomous agent.

The concept of nested autonomy is based on the idea of delegation and division of tasks. In a nested system, higher-level agents are responsible for overseeing and coordinating lower-level agents. This allows for a more efficient and effective management of tasks, as each agent is only responsible for a specific set of tasks.

One of the key advantages of the nested autonomy paradigm is its ability to handle dynamic and uncertain environments. In traditional single-vehicle autonomy, a system may struggle to adapt to changes in the environment or unexpected events. However, in a nested system, each agent is responsible for a specific task, and can adapt to changes in their own environment. This allows for a more robust and resilient system.

The nested autonomy paradigm also allows for scalability and flexibility. As systems become more complex and dynamic, the number and complexity of agents can be increased to handle the additional tasks and responsibilities. This allows for the system to adapt and grow as needed.

In the next section, we will explore the principles behind nested autonomy and how it can be applied in marine autonomy. We will also discuss the advantages and limitations of this approach, as well as its potential applications. 





### Subsection: 8.1b Basic Nested Autonomy Paradigm

The nested autonomy paradigm is a powerful approach to designing and implementing marine autonomous systems. It allows for a more efficient and effective management of complex systems, as well as the ability to handle dynamic and uncertain environments. In this section, we will explore the basic principles behind the nested autonomy paradigm and how it can be applied to marine autonomous systems.

#### 8.1b.1 Principles of Nested Autonomy

The nested autonomy paradigm is based on the concept of delegation and division of tasks. In a nested system, higher-level agents are responsible for overseeing and coordinating lower-level agents. This allows for a more efficient and effective management of tasks, as each agent is only responsible for a specific set of tasks.

The nested autonomy paradigm also allows for scalability and flexibility. As systems become more complex and dynamic, the number and complexity of agents can be increased to handle the additional tasks and responsibilities. This allows for the system to adapt and grow as needed.

#### 8.1b.2 Implementing Nested Autonomy in Marine Autonomous Systems

The nested autonomy paradigm can be applied to marine autonomous systems in a variety of ways. One common approach is to use a hierarchical structure, where higher-level agents are responsible for overseeing and coordinating lower-level agents. This allows for a more efficient and effective management of tasks, as each agent is only responsible for a specific set of tasks.

Another approach is to use a decentralized system, where each agent is responsible for a specific task and communicates with other agents to coordinate their actions. This allows for a more flexible and adaptable system, as each agent can make decisions based on their own information and communicate with other agents to coordinate their actions.

#### 8.1b.3 Challenges and Considerations

While the nested autonomy paradigm offers many advantages, there are also some challenges and considerations to keep in mind. One challenge is the potential for communication and coordination issues between agents. As systems become more complex and dynamic, ensuring effective communication and coordination between agents can be a challenge.

Another consideration is the need for robust and reliable sensors and communication systems. In order for nested autonomy to be effective, marine autonomous systems must be equipped with sensors and communication systems that can accurately and reliably gather and transmit information. This requires careful design and testing to ensure the system's performance and reliability.

#### 8.1b.4 Future Directions

The nested autonomy paradigm is a rapidly evolving field, and there are many opportunities for future research and development. One area of interest is the integration of artificial intuition into nested autonomy systems. Artificial intuition, which allows for decision-making based on intuition rather than explicit knowledge, could greatly enhance the capabilities of marine autonomous systems.

Another direction for future research is the development of more advanced learning algorithms for nested autonomy systems. These algorithms could allow for more efficient and effective learning and adaptation, leading to improved performance in dynamic and uncertain environments.

In conclusion, the nested autonomy paradigm offers a powerful approach to designing and implementing marine autonomous systems. By delegating and dividing tasks, allowing for scalability and flexibility, and incorporating advanced learning algorithms, nested autonomy can greatly enhance the capabilities of marine autonomous systems. As technology continues to advance, the potential for nested autonomy in marine applications is vast.





### Subsection: 8.1c Advanced Nested Autonomy Paradigm

In the previous section, we discussed the basic principles and implementation of the nested autonomy paradigm in marine autonomous systems. In this section, we will explore some advanced concepts and techniques that can be used to further enhance the capabilities of nested autonomy.

#### 8.1c.1 Advanced Delegation and Division of Tasks

In the basic nested autonomy paradigm, higher-level agents are responsible for overseeing and coordinating lower-level agents. However, in more complex systems, it may be beneficial to have multiple levels of agents, with each level responsible for a different set of tasks. This allows for a more granular and specialized management of tasks, leading to improved efficiency and effectiveness.

#### 8.1c.2 Dynamic Adaptation and Learning

One of the key advantages of nested autonomy is its ability to adapt and grow as needed. This can be further enhanced by incorporating dynamic adaptation and learning techniques. For example, agents can learn from their experiences and adjust their behavior and tasks in response to changing environments or goals. This allows for a more flexible and adaptable system.

#### 8.1c.3 Collaborative Problem Solving

In addition to delegation and division of tasks, the nested autonomy paradigm can also be used for collaborative problem solving. By having multiple agents working together towards a common goal, complex tasks can be broken down and solved more efficiently. This can be particularly useful in marine autonomous systems, where tasks may be highly complex and require the coordination of multiple agents.

#### 8.1c.4 Integration with Other Technologies

The nested autonomy paradigm can also be integrated with other technologies, such as artificial intuition and lifelong planning A*. This allows for a more holistic approach to autonomous systems, where different technologies work together to achieve a common goal. For example, artificial intuition can be used to make decisions and plan tasks, while lifelong planning A* can be used for long-term goal planning and adaptation.

#### 8.1c.5 Ethical Considerations

As with any technology, there are ethical considerations that must be taken into account when implementing nested autonomy in marine autonomous systems. This includes issues such as accountability, transparency, and potential biases in decision-making. It is important to carefully consider these ethical implications and address them in the design and implementation of nested autonomy systems.

In conclusion, the nested autonomy paradigm is a powerful approach to designing and implementing marine autonomous systems. By incorporating advanced concepts and techniques, such as dynamic adaptation, collaborative problem solving, and integration with other technologies, the capabilities of nested autonomy can be further enhanced. However, it is important to also consider and address ethical implications to ensure responsible and ethical use of this technology.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the concept of nested autonomy in marine systems. We have discussed the benefits of using a hierarchical approach to autonomy, where lower-level agents are responsible for basic tasks while higher-level agents handle more complex decisions. We have also examined the challenges and limitations of nested autonomy, such as the need for effective communication and coordination between different levels of autonomy.

Overall, nested autonomy offers a promising solution for managing the complexity of marine systems. By delegating tasks and decisions to different levels of autonomy, we can achieve more efficient and effective control of these systems. However, it is important to carefully design and implement nested autonomy systems to ensure that they are robust and reliable in real-world scenarios.

## Chapter: Marine Autonomy, Sensing and Communications: A Comprehensive Guide

### Introduction

In recent years, there has been a growing interest in the development of autonomous marine systems. These systems are designed to operate independently, without human intervention, and are equipped with advanced sensing and communication capabilities. The use of autonomous marine systems has the potential to revolutionize various industries, including ocean exploration, research, and defense. However, the successful implementation of these systems requires a deep understanding of the underlying technologies and their interactions.

In this chapter, we will explore the concept of nested control in marine autonomy. Nested control is a hierarchical approach to controlling autonomous systems, where higher levels of control are responsible for making decisions and coordinating lower levels. This approach is crucial for managing the complexity of autonomous marine systems and ensuring their efficient operation. We will discuss the principles of nested control, its applications in marine autonomy, and the challenges and opportunities it presents.

Furthermore, we will delve into the role of sensing and communication in nested control. Sensing is the process of collecting information about the environment, while communication is the exchange of information between different components of the system. Both sensing and communication are essential for the successful operation of autonomous marine systems. We will explore the various sensing and communication techniques used in nested control and their impact on system performance.

Finally, we will discuss the future prospects of nested control in marine autonomy. As technology continues to advance, the potential for autonomous marine systems is vast. We will examine the potential applications of nested control in emerging fields such as underwater robotics and autonomous surface vessels. Additionally, we will discuss the challenges and opportunities that lie ahead in the development and implementation of nested control in marine autonomy.

In conclusion, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to nested control in marine autonomy. By understanding the principles, applications, and challenges of nested control, we can pave the way for the successful implementation of autonomous marine systems and their potential impact on various industries.

#### 9.1a Introduction to Autonomous Collaborative Search

Autonomous Collaborative Search (ACS) is a technology that enables a group of autonomous vehicles to work together to achieve a common goal. This technology is particularly relevant in the context of marine operations, where it can enhance search and rescue operations, environmental monitoring, and resource exploration. The collective capabilities of the autonomous vehicles allow them to cover a larger area and perform tasks more efficiently than a single vehicle.

In this section, we will provide an overview of ACS, discussing its principles, applications, and challenges. We will also explore the various aspects of ACS, including the communication protocols used, the algorithms for task allocation and coordination, and the challenges associated with operating in a dynamic and unpredictable marine environment.

#### 9.1b Principles of Autonomous Collaborative Search

The principles of ACS are rooted in the concepts of autonomy, collaboration, and search. Autonomy refers to the ability of an autonomous vehicle to make decisions and perform tasks without human intervention. Collaboration involves the coordination of multiple autonomous vehicles to achieve a common goal. Search, in the context of ACS, refers to the process of exploring an area to find a target or information.

The principles of ACS are implemented through a combination of hardware, software, and algorithms. The hardware includes sensors, communication devices, and processing units. The software includes operating systems, control algorithms, and communication protocols. The algorithms are used for tasks such as target detection, tracking, and task allocation.

#### 9.1c Applications of Autonomous Collaborative Search

ACS has a wide range of applications in marine operations. One of the most significant applications is in search and rescue operations. ACS can enable a group of autonomous vehicles to cover a larger area and search more efficiently than a single vehicle. This can significantly increase the chances of finding a missing person or vessel.

ACS can also be used for environmental monitoring. A group of autonomous vehicles can collect data on ocean temperature, salinity, and other environmental parameters over a large area. This can provide valuable information for understanding climate change, ocean currents, and other environmental phenomena.

Another application of ACS is in resource exploration. ACS can enable a group of autonomous vehicles to explore a large area for resources such as oil, gas, or minerals. This can be more efficient and cost-effective than using a single vehicle or a human-operated fleet.

#### 9.1d Challenges of Autonomous Collaborative Search

Despite its potential benefits, ACS also presents several challenges. One of the main challenges is the development of robust and reliable communication protocols. These protocols must ensure that multiple autonomous vehicles can communicate effectively and efficiently, even in challenging marine environments.

Another challenge is the development of effective algorithms for task allocation and coordination. These algorithms must be able to distribute tasks among the autonomous vehicles in a way that maximizes efficiency and minimizes conflicts.

Operating in a dynamic and unpredictable marine environment also presents significant challenges. The marine environment can change rapidly due to factors such as weather conditions, ocean currents, and marine traffic. This can make it difficult for the autonomous vehicles to perform their tasks effectively.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these challenges and discuss potential solutions. We will also explore the current state of the art in ACS and discuss future directions for research and development.

#### 9.1e Future Directions in Autonomous Collaborative Search

As the technology of autonomous vehicles continues to advance, so too will the potential for autonomous collaborative search. One of the most promising areas of research is in the development of artificial intelligence (AI) systems for autonomous vehicles. These AI systems could be used to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of ACS, particularly in terms of task allocation and coordination.

Another area of research is in the development of advanced communication protocols. These protocols could use technologies such as 5G and Internet of Things (IoT) to enable more robust and reliable communication between autonomous vehicles. This could significantly improve the efficiency of ACS operations, particularly in challenging marine environments.

In addition, there is ongoing research into the use of unmanned aerial vehicles (UAVs) for ACS. These UAVs could provide aerial surveillance and reconnaissance, complementing the work of autonomous surface vehicles (ASVs) and autonomous underwater vehicles (AUVs). This could significantly enhance the capabilities of ACS, particularly in terms of search and rescue operations.

Finally, there is ongoing research into the use of blockchain technology for ACS. Blockchain could be used to securely store and share data between autonomous vehicles, improving the efficiency and effectiveness of ACS operations. This could also help to address concerns about privacy and security in the use of ACS.

In conclusion, the future of autonomous collaborative search is bright, with many exciting areas of research and development. As these technologies continue to advance, so too will the capabilities of ACS, enabling more efficient and effective marine operations.

#### 9.1c Applications of Autonomous Collaborative Search

Autonomous Collaborative Search (ACS) has a wide range of applications in marine operations. These applications are not only limited to search and rescue operations but also extend to environmental monitoring, resource exploration, and military operations. 

##### Search and Rescue Operations

One of the most significant applications of ACS is in search and rescue operations. The ability of autonomous vehicles to collaborate and cover a larger area can significantly increase the chances of finding a missing person or vessel. For instance, in the case of a shipwreck, a group of autonomous surface vehicles (ASVs) and autonomous underwater vehicles (AUVs) can work together to search the surface and underwater areas. The ASVs can use their sensors to detect any surface debris, while the AUVs can search the underwater area for any signs of the wreckage. This collaborative approach can be more efficient and effective than using a single vehicle or a human-operated fleet.

##### Environmental Monitoring

ACS can also be used for environmental monitoring. A group of autonomous vehicles can collect data on ocean temperature, salinity, and other environmental parameters over a large area. This can provide valuable information for understanding climate change, ocean currents, and other environmental phenomena. For example, a group of ASVs and AUVs can work together to collect data on ocean temperature and salinity over a large area. This data can then be used to create a detailed map of the ocean, which can be used for various purposes such as predicting weather patterns and understanding ocean currents.

##### Resource Exploration

Another application of ACS is in resource exploration. ACS can enable a group of autonomous vehicles to explore a large area for resources such as oil, gas, or minerals. This can be more efficient and cost-effective than using a single vehicle or a human-operated fleet. For instance, a group of ASVs and AUVs can work together to explore a large area for oil deposits. The ASVs can use their sensors to detect any surface signs of oil, while the AUVs can search the underwater area for any signs of oil deposits. This collaborative approach can significantly increase the chances of finding a resource deposit.

##### Military Operations

ACS also has applications in military operations. Autonomous vehicles can be used for surveillance and reconnaissance missions, which can be dangerous for human soldiers. A group of autonomous vehicles can work together to cover a larger area and provide real-time information about the enemy's movements. This can help the military to make more informed decisions and improve the chances of success in a military operation.

In conclusion, ACS has a wide range of applications in marine operations. Its ability to enable a group of autonomous vehicles to work together can significantly increase the efficiency and effectiveness of various operations, from search and rescue to resource exploration and military operations. As the technology continues to advance, we can expect to see even more applications of ACS in the future.




#### 9.1b Basic Autonomous Collaborative Search

The basic principle of Autonomous Collaborative Search (ACS) involves the coordination of multiple autonomous vehicles to achieve a common goal. This is achieved through a combination of hardware, software, and algorithms. The hardware includes sensors, communication devices, and processing units. The software includes operating systems, control algorithms, and communication protocols. The algorithms are used for tasks such as target detection, tracking, and task allocation.

The basic ACS process begins with the initialization of the search area. This is typically done by defining a search region and dividing it into smaller subregions. Each subregion is then assigned to a specific autonomous vehicle. The vehicles then start searching their assigned subregions simultaneously.

As the vehicles search their assigned subregions, they communicate with each other to share information about their findings. This information can include the presence of targets, the location of these targets, and the quality of the information. This information is then used to update the search area and adjust the search strategy.

The search continues until all the subregions have been searched or until a certain condition is met. This condition could be the detection of a target, the exhaustion of resources, or a predefined time limit. Once the condition is met, the vehicles coordinate to report their findings and terminate the search.

The basic ACS process can be represented mathematically as follows:

Let $N$ be the number of vehicles, $R$ be the search region, and $S_i$ be the subregion assigned to vehicle $i$. The search process can be represented as:

$$
\forall i \in \{1, ..., N\}: S_i \subseteq R
$$

The communication process can be represented as:

$$
\forall i, j \in \{1, ..., N\}: i \neq j \Rightarrow C_{ij} \subseteq S_i \cap S_j
$$

where $C_{ij}$ is the communication area between vehicles $i$ and $j$.

The update process can be represented as:

$$
\forall i \in \{1, ..., N\}: U_i \subseteq S_i \setminus C_i
$$

where $U_i$ is the updated search area for vehicle $i$.

The termination process can be represented as:

$$
\exists i \in \{1, ..., N\}: T_i = true
$$

where $T_i$ is the termination condition for vehicle $i$.

In the next section, we will discuss the various aspects of ACS in more detail, including the communication protocols used, the algorithms for task allocation and coordination, and the challenges associated with operating in a dynamic and unpredictable marine environment.

#### 9.1c Advanced Autonomous Collaborative Search

Advanced Autonomous Collaborative Search (ACS) builds upon the basic principles of ACS, incorporating more complex algorithms and strategies to enhance the efficiency and effectiveness of the search process. This section will delve into the advanced aspects of ACS, including advanced search strategies, advanced communication protocols, and advanced task allocation algorithms.

##### Advanced Search Strategies

Advanced search strategies in ACS involve the use of more sophisticated algorithms for target detection, tracking, and task allocation. These algorithms can be based on machine learning techniques, such as neural networks or decision trees, which can learn from past experiences and adapt to changing conditions. For example, a neural network can be trained to detect targets based on a combination of sensor data, such as acoustics, imagery, and infrared.

Another advanced search strategy is the use of implicit data structures, which can be used to represent and process large amounts of data efficiently. These structures can be particularly useful in ACS, where large amounts of data can be generated by the sensors of multiple vehicles.

##### Advanced Communication Protocols

Advanced communication protocols in ACS involve the use of more complex communication schemes, such as multimedia web ontology language (MOWL) or the Simple Function Point method. These protocols can facilitate the exchange of more detailed and structured information between vehicles, which can improve the accuracy and efficiency of the search process.

For example, the Simple Function Point method can be used to define and measure the complexity of the search task, which can be useful for task allocation and coordination. Similarly, MOWL can be used to define and process ontologies, which can be useful for representing and reasoning about the search task and its context.

##### Advanced Task Allocation Algorithms

Advanced task allocation algorithms in ACS involve the use of more sophisticated algorithms for assigning tasks to vehicles. These algorithms can take into account various factors, such as the capabilities of the vehicles, the complexity of the task, and the communication and coordination requirements.

For example, the Remez algorithm can be used to allocate tasks in a way that minimizes the overall search time. This algorithm involves the use of implicit data structures and multiset operations, which can be particularly useful in ACS.

In conclusion, advanced ACS involves the use of advanced search strategies, communication protocols, and task allocation algorithms. These advanced aspects can significantly enhance the efficiency and effectiveness of the search process, making ACS a powerful tool for marine operations.




#### 9.1c Advanced Autonomous Collaborative Search

Advanced Autonomous Collaborative Search (ACS) builds upon the basic principles of ACS, incorporating advanced techniques and algorithms to enhance the efficiency and effectiveness of the search process. These advanced techniques include machine learning, artificial intelligence, and advanced communication protocols.

Machine learning algorithms can be used to improve the accuracy of target detection and tracking. These algorithms can learn from past experiences and adapt to changing environments, making them particularly suited for autonomous search and rescue operations. For example, a machine learning algorithm could be trained on a dataset of past search and rescue missions, learning to recognize patterns and features associated with targets. This could significantly improve the accuracy of target detection and tracking, reducing the time and resources required for the search.

Artificial intelligence can be used to automate the decision-making process in ACS. This could involve the use of decision trees, fuzzy logic, or other AI techniques to make decisions about the search strategy based on the information collected by the vehicles. For example, if a vehicle detects a target, it could use AI to decide whether to report the target immediately or continue searching for a certain period of time. This could help to optimize the search process, reducing the time and resources required to find a target.

Advanced communication protocols can be used to improve the efficiency of communication between vehicles. These protocols could include techniques such as multi-hop communication, where vehicles relay information to each other, or adaptive communication, where the communication strategy is adjusted based on the current conditions. These protocols could help to reduce the communication overhead and improve the reliability of communication, particularly in challenging environments.

The advanced ACS process can be represented mathematically as follows:

Let $N$ be the number of vehicles, $R$ be the search region, and $S_i$ be the subregion assigned to vehicle $i$. The search process can be represented as:

$$
\forall i \in \{1, ..., N\}: S_i \subseteq R
$$

The communication process can be represented as:

$$
\forall i, j \in \{1, ..., N\}: i \neq j \Rightarrow C_{ij} \subseteq S_i \cap S_j
$$

where $C_{ij}$ is the communication area between vehicles $i$ and $j$.

The update process can be represented as:

$$
\forall i \in \{1, ..., N\}: U_i = \bigcup_{j \in \{1, ..., N\}: j \neq i} C_{ij}
$$

where $U_i$ is the updated search area for vehicle $i$.

The decision-making process can be represented as:

$$
\forall i \in \{1, ..., N\}: D_i = f(I_i, U_i)
$$

where $D_i$ is the decision made by vehicle $i$, $I_i$ is the information collected by vehicle $i$, and $f$ is the decision-making function.

### Introduction

We hope that this chapter will not only inform readers about the current state of underwater imaging but also inspire them to explore this exciting field further. So let us dive in and explore the world of underwater imaging with Dr. Balasuriya.




### Section: 10.1 Underwater Imaging:

Underwater imaging is a crucial aspect of marine autonomy, allowing us to capture images of the underwater environment and analyze it for various purposes. In this section, we will explore the principles of underwater imaging, specifically focusing on acoustics.

#### 10.1a Introduction to Underwater Imaging

Underwater imaging is the process of capturing images of the underwater environment. This can be done using various techniques, including optical imaging systems and imaging sonars. Optical imaging systems have been successfully used in deep ocean searches, either independently or in combination with a side-scan sonar. The obvious advantage of an optical imaging system is that the image produced can result in identification of the target without the need for time-consuming contact classification. However, these systems are limited by the attenuation of light and backscattering, which can degrade the image quality.

On the other hand, imaging sonars have gained resolution and accessibility, delivering better images. These sonars are used to produce complete maps of regions of the sea floor by stitching together sequences of sonar images. However, imaging sonar images often lack proper contrast and are degraded by artefacts and distortions due to noise, attitude changes of the AUV/ROV carrying the sonar, or non-uniform beam patterns.

One of the key challenges in underwater imaging is the assumption that corresponding pixels should have the same color. However, this cannot be guaranteed in an underwater scene due to dispersion and backscatter. To address this issue, it is possible to digitally model this phenomenon and create a virtual image with those effects removed.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the principles of acoustics and how it is used in underwater imaging. We will also explore the research interests of Dr. Balasuriya in this field and how it has contributed to the development of underwater imaging technology. 

#### 10.1b Underwater Imaging Techniques

There are several techniques used in underwater imaging, each with its own advantages and limitations. In this section, we will explore some of the most commonly used techniques, including optical imaging systems, imaging sonars, and acoustics.

##### Optical Imaging Systems

Optical imaging systems have been successfully used in deep ocean searches, either independently or in combination with a side-scan sonar. These systems rely on conventional strobe or floodlights as their illumination source. However, due to limitations imposed by attenuation of the light and backscattering, still and video sensors need to be within 10 to 20 meters of a target to identify it.

One of the main advantages of optical imaging systems is that the image produced can result in identification of the target without the need for time-consuming contact classification. However, these systems are limited by the attenuation of light and backscattering, which can degrade the image quality.

##### Imaging Sonars

Imaging sonars have gained resolution and accessibility, delivering better images. These sonars are used to produce complete maps of regions of the sea floor by stitching together sequences of sonar images. However, imaging sonar images often lack proper contrast and are degraded by artefacts and distortions due to noise, attitude changes of the AUV/ROV carrying the sonar, or non-uniform beam patterns.

One of the key challenges in imaging sonars is the assumption that corresponding pixels should have the same color. However, this cannot be guaranteed in an underwater scene due to dispersion and backscatter. To address this issue, it is possible to digitally model this phenomenon and create a virtual image with those effects removed.

##### Acoustics

Acoustics is a crucial aspect of underwater imaging, allowing us to capture images of the underwater environment. In this section, we will explore the principles of acoustics and how it is used in underwater imaging.

Acoustics is the branch of physics that deals with sound, which is a form of mechanical wave. In underwater imaging, acoustics is used to create images of the underwater environment. This is done by sending out sound waves and measuring the time it takes for the waves to bounce back, providing information about the objects in the water.

One of the key advantages of acoustics is that it is not affected by attenuation of light and backscattering, making it a reliable technique for imaging in deep ocean environments. However, acoustics also has its limitations, such as the inability to capture high-resolution images and the need for specialized equipment.

In the next section, we will explore the research interests of Dr. Balasuriya in underwater imaging, specifically focusing on acoustics. We will also discuss how his research has contributed to the development of underwater imaging technology.

#### 10.1c Applications in Marine Autonomy

Underwater imaging plays a crucial role in marine autonomy, providing a means for autonomous vehicles to capture images of the underwater environment and analyze it for various purposes. In this section, we will explore some of the key applications of underwater imaging in marine autonomy.

##### Underwater Imaging for Autonomous Vehicles

Autonomous vehicles, such as underwater gliders and remotely operated vehicles (ROVs), rely on underwater imaging for navigation, mapping, and target detection. These vehicles use optical imaging systems and imaging sonars to capture images of the underwater environment. The images are then processed and analyzed to determine the vehicle's position, identify underwater features, and detect targets of interest.

One of the key challenges in using underwater imaging for autonomous vehicles is the need for accurate and reliable image processing algorithms. These algorithms must be able to handle the challenges posed by the underwater environment, such as attenuation of light and backscatter, to accurately interpret the images and extract useful information.

##### Underwater Imaging for Underwater Exploration

Underwater imaging is also used for underwater exploration, allowing researchers to study and understand the underwater environment without the need for physical intervention. This is particularly useful in deep ocean environments, where traditional methods of exploration, such as manned submersibles, are not feasible.

Underwater imaging techniques, such as optical imaging systems and imaging sonars, are used to capture images of the underwater environment. These images are then processed and analyzed to identify underwater features, such as seafloor topography and marine life, and to study their behavior and interactions.

##### Underwater Imaging for Underwater Sensing

Underwater imaging is also used for underwater sensing, providing a means for autonomous vehicles to sense and detect changes in the underwater environment. This is particularly useful for tasks such as underwater mapping and monitoring, where the vehicle needs to continuously sense and update its understanding of the environment.

Underwater imaging techniques, such as acoustics and optical imaging systems, are used to sense changes in the underwater environment. These changes can then be used to update the vehicle's map of the environment, allowing it to navigate and perform tasks more effectively.

In conclusion, underwater imaging plays a crucial role in marine autonomy, providing a means for autonomous vehicles to capture images of the underwater environment and analyze it for various purposes. As technology continues to advance, we can expect to see even more applications of underwater imaging in marine autonomy.

We have learned about the unique challenges and opportunities presented by the underwater environment, and how underwater imaging can help us overcome these challenges. We have also discussed the various techniques and technologies used in underwater imaging, including sonar, acoustics, and optical imaging.

Underwater imaging is a crucial component of marine autonomy, enabling autonomous vehicles to navigate, map, and explore the underwater environment. It allows us to gather valuable data about the ocean, from its topography and marine life to its currents and pollution levels. With the continued advancement of technology, underwater imaging will play an increasingly important role in our understanding and management of the ocean.

As we conclude this chapter, it is important to remember that underwater imaging is not just about the technology. It is also about the people behind it, the researchers and engineers who are constantly pushing the boundaries of what is possible. Their dedication and innovation are what drive the field of underwater imaging forward, and it is their work that will ultimately shape the future of marine autonomy.

## Chapter: Chapter 11: Guest Lecture: Dr. Bahl, Underwater Acoustics

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of underwater acoustics, a crucial aspect of marine autonomy. We will be joined by Dr. Bahl, a renowned expert in the field, who will provide a guest lecture on the topic. Underwater acoustics is a complex and multifaceted field that deals with the generation, propagation, and reception of sound waves in the underwater environment. It plays a vital role in various applications such as underwater navigation, communication, and sensing.

The chapter will begin with an overview of underwater acoustics, providing a broad understanding of the field and its importance in marine autonomy. We will then delve into the specifics of underwater acoustics, exploring the unique characteristics of sound propagation in water, the effects of water properties on sound, and the challenges of underwater acoustics. 

Dr. Bahl's guest lecture will provide a deeper insight into the topic, offering a comprehensive understanding of the principles and applications of underwater acoustics. We will explore the latest advancements in the field, including the use of acoustics in underwater imaging and the development of acoustic modems for underwater communication. 

The chapter will also discuss the challenges and future directions of underwater acoustics, including the impact of noise and interference, the development of new acoustic materials, and the integration of acoustics with other sensing modalities. 

By the end of this chapter, readers will have a solid understanding of underwater acoustics and its role in marine autonomy. They will also gain insights into the latest developments and future directions of the field, providing them with a foundation for further exploration and research in this exciting area.




### Section: 10.1b Basic Underwater Imaging

Underwater imaging is a complex process that involves capturing images of the underwater environment. This can be done using various techniques, including optical imaging systems and imaging sonars. In this section, we will delve deeper into the basic principles of underwater imaging, focusing on the role of acoustics and the challenges faced in this field.

#### 10.1b.1 Acoustics in Underwater Imaging

Acoustics plays a crucial role in underwater imaging, particularly in imaging sonars. Sonar, short for sound navigation and ranging, is a technique that uses sound waves to navigate, communicate, and detect objects underwater. Imaging sonar, on the other hand, uses sound waves to create images of the underwater environment.

The basic principle of imaging sonar is similar to that of optical imaging systems. Both techniques aim to capture an image of the underwater environment. However, while optical imaging systems rely on light, imaging sonar uses sound waves. This is particularly useful in underwater environments where light is limited due to depth and turbidity.

#### 10.1b.2 Challenges in Underwater Imaging

Despite the advantages of imaging sonar, there are several challenges that need to be addressed. One of the main challenges is the assumption that corresponding pixels should have the same color. However, this cannot be guaranteed in an underwater scene due to dispersion and backscatter. Dispersion refers to the spreading of light or sound waves due to variations in the refractive index of the medium. Backscatter, on the other hand, refers to the reflection of sound waves back in the direction they came from.

To address these challenges, it is possible to digitally model the effects of dispersion and backscatter and create a virtual image with those effects removed. This involves using advanced image processing techniques to remove the effects of dispersion and backscatter from the captured image.

#### 10.1b.3 Future Directions

The field of underwater imaging is constantly evolving, with researchers continually working to improve the quality of images captured and the efficiency of the imaging process. One promising direction is the use of machine learning techniques to improve image quality. Machine learning algorithms can be trained to recognize and remove the effects of dispersion and backscatter from captured images, improving the overall quality of the image.

Another direction is the development of new imaging techniques that can overcome the limitations of current imaging systems. For example, researchers are exploring the use of mid-infrared (MIR) imaging for underwater applications. MIR imaging has several advantages over traditional imaging techniques, including the ability to penetrate deeper into the water column and the ability to image through sediment and other particles.

In conclusion, underwater imaging is a complex field that involves capturing images of the underwater environment. Acoustics plays a crucial role in this field, particularly in imaging sonar. However, there are several challenges that need to be addressed, including the effects of dispersion and backscatter. With ongoing research and development, it is likely that these challenges will be overcome, leading to improved image quality and more efficient imaging techniques.




### Section: 10.1c Advanced Underwater Imaging

In this section, we will delve deeper into the advanced techniques used in underwater imaging. These techniques aim to overcome the challenges faced in basic underwater imaging and provide more accurate and detailed images of the underwater environment.

#### 10.1c.1 Advanced Imaging Sonar

Advanced imaging sonar techniques aim to overcome the limitations of basic imaging sonar. One such technique is the use of multiple frequencies. By using multiple frequencies, it is possible to create a more detailed image of the underwater environment. This is because different frequencies penetrate the water at different depths, allowing for a more comprehensive view of the underwater environment.

Another advanced imaging sonar technique is the use of synthetic aperture sonar. This technique involves moving the sonar transducer along the water column and combining the received signals to create a high-resolution image. This technique is particularly useful in imaging large objects, such as ships or submarines.

#### 10.1c.2 Advanced Optical Imaging

Advanced optical imaging techniques aim to overcome the limitations of basic optical imaging systems. One such technique is the use of laser-imaging systems. These systems use a blue/green laser as the illumination source, which minimizes attenuation and backscattering problems. This allows for the imaging of targets as far as 50 meters away.

Another advanced optical imaging technique is the use of hyperspectral imaging. This technique involves capturing images at different wavelengths, allowing for a more detailed analysis of the underwater environment. This can be particularly useful in identifying different types of marine life or detecting changes in the water quality.

#### 10.1c.3 Challenges in Advanced Underwater Imaging

Despite the advancements in underwater imaging techniques, there are still several challenges that need to be addressed. One of the main challenges is the need for more accurate and detailed imaging. This requires the development of more advanced imaging systems and techniques.

Another challenge is the need for more efficient data processing. With the increasing use of advanced imaging techniques, there is a growing amount of data that needs to be processed. This requires the development of more efficient algorithms and software tools.

In conclusion, advanced underwater imaging techniques aim to overcome the limitations of basic imaging systems and provide more accurate and detailed images of the underwater environment. However, there are still several challenges that need to be addressed to further improve the capabilities of underwater imaging.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we had the opportunity to learn from Dr. Balasuriya about the fascinating world of underwater imaging. We explored the various techniques and technologies used in this field, including sonar, lidar, and optical imaging. We also discussed the challenges and limitations of underwater imaging, such as water turbidity and signal interference.

Dr. Balasuriya's lecture highlighted the importance of underwater imaging in marine autonomy. With the increasing demand for autonomous underwater vehicles (AUVs) and unmanned surface vehicles (USVs), the ability to accurately and efficiently image the underwater environment is crucial. Underwater imaging not only allows us to gather valuable data about the ocean, but also enables us to make informed decisions about marine operations and resource management.

As we continue to advance in the field of marine autonomy, it is important to further develop and improve underwater imaging techniques. This will not only enhance our understanding of the ocean, but also aid in the development of more efficient and effective marine operations.

## Chapter: Marine Autonomy, Sensing and Communications: A Comprehensive Guide

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will explore the topic of marine autonomy, sensing, and communications. Marine autonomy refers to the ability of a vessel or system to operate independently without human intervention. This includes the use of advanced technologies such as artificial intelligence and machine learning to make decisions and navigate through the ocean. Sensing is a crucial aspect of marine autonomy, as it allows vessels to gather information about their surroundings and make informed decisions. Communications, on the other hand, play a vital role in enabling vessels to communicate with each other and with shore-based operations.

The use of marine autonomy, sensing, and communications has become increasingly important in recent years due to the growing demand for efficient and sustainable transportation in the ocean. With the increasing traffic in the world's oceans, there is a need for vessels to operate autonomously and efficiently, reducing the need for human intervention and minimizing environmental impact.

In this chapter, we will cover various topics related to marine autonomy, sensing, and communications. We will discuss the different types of marine autonomy systems, including unmanned surface vehicles (USVs), autonomous underwater vehicles (AUVs), and autonomous surface ships (ASVs). We will also explore the various sensors used for marine autonomy, such as radar, sonar, and optical sensors. Additionally, we will delve into the different types of communication systems used in marine autonomy, including satellite communication, acoustic communication, and ad hoc networks.

Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to marine autonomy, sensing, and communications. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a better understanding of the current state of marine autonomy and the role it plays in the future of ocean transportation.

## Chapter 11: Underwater Imaging:




### Introduction

In this chapter, we will explore the topic of contact related behaviors and dynamic behavior spawning in the context of marine autonomy, sensing, and communications. This is a crucial aspect of marine robotics, as it involves the ability of autonomous marine vehicles to interact with their environment and other entities in a dynamic and adaptive manner.

Contact related behaviors refer to the actions and responses of an autonomous marine vehicle when it comes into contact with other objects or entities. This could include physical contact, such as colliding with a ship or underwater structure, or non-physical contact, such as detecting the presence of another vehicle or marine life. The behavior of the vehicle in these situations is crucial for its safety and effectiveness in carrying out its mission.

Dynamic behavior spawning, on the other hand, refers to the ability of an autonomous marine vehicle to generate new behaviors or actions in response to changes in its environment or mission objectives. This could include adapting to changes in weather conditions, navigating through complex underwater terrain, or responding to unexpected obstacles. The ability to spawn new behaviors is essential for the robustness and adaptability of marine autonomous systems.

Throughout this chapter, we will delve into the various aspects of contact related behaviors and dynamic behavior spawning, including the underlying principles, techniques, and applications. We will also discuss the challenges and future directions in this field, as well as provide examples and case studies to illustrate these concepts. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of these important topics and their role in marine autonomy, sensing, and communications.




### Subsection: 11.1a Introduction to Dynamic Behavior Spawning

Dynamic behavior spawning is a crucial aspect of marine autonomy, sensing, and communications. It involves the ability of an autonomous marine vehicle to generate new behaviors or actions in response to changes in its environment or mission objectives. This ability is essential for the robustness and adaptability of marine autonomous systems.

#### 11.1a.1 Principles of Dynamic Behavior Spawning

The principles of dynamic behavior spawning are based on the concept of hierarchical finite-state machines (HFSMs). Each actor in a system, such as an autonomous marine vehicle, has a behavior defined by a HFSM. This behavior is a directed graph consisting of nodes called "states" and edges called "transitions". State transitions are triggered by incoming messages from an internal or external end port.

During the state change, certain pieces of code are executed. The programmer can attach these codes to the states and transitions. In the case of marine autonomy, these codes are often responsible for generating new behaviors or actions. For example, if an autonomous marine vehicle detects a change in its environment, such as a sudden increase in water current, it may need to spawn a new behavior to adapt to this change.

#### 11.1a.2 Techniques for Dynamic Behavior Spawning

There are several techniques for dynamic behavior spawning, each with its own advantages and limitations. One common technique is the use of implicit data structures, which allow for efficient storage and retrieval of data. This can be particularly useful for large-scale marine autonomy systems, where the ability to quickly access and process data is crucial.

Another technique is the use of artificial intuition, which involves the use of machine learning algorithms to generate new behaviors or actions. This approach can be particularly useful for complex and dynamic environments, where traditional rule-based approaches may not be sufficient.

#### 11.1a.3 Applications of Dynamic Behavior Spawning

Dynamic behavior spawning has a wide range of applications in marine autonomy. It is particularly useful for tasks that involve interaction with the environment, such as navigation, obstacle avoidance, and target tracking. It can also be used for tasks that involve interaction with other entities, such as communication and coordination with other autonomous vehicles.

In addition, dynamic behavior spawning can be used for tasks that involve adaptability and robustness, such as adapting to changes in mission objectives or unexpected events. This makes it a crucial aspect of marine autonomy, as it allows for the system to respond effectively to changes in its environment or mission objectives.

#### 11.1a.4 Challenges and Future Directions

Despite its many advantages, dynamic behavior spawning also presents several challenges. One of the main challenges is the complexity of the systems involved, which can make it difficult to design and implement effective spawning strategies. Another challenge is the need for continuous learning and adaptation, as the environment and mission objectives can change rapidly.

In the future, advancements in machine learning and artificial intelligence are expected to address these challenges and improve the effectiveness of dynamic behavior spawning. In addition, the use of implicit data structures and other techniques will continue to play a crucial role in the development of efficient and robust marine autonomy systems.

### Conclusion

Dynamic behavior spawning is a crucial aspect of marine autonomy, sensing, and communications. It allows for the generation of new behaviors or actions in response to changes in the environment or mission objectives, making it essential for the robustness and adaptability of marine autonomous systems. With the continued advancements in technology and research, we can expect to see even more sophisticated and effective techniques for dynamic behavior spawning in the future.





#### 11.1b Basic Dynamic Behavior Spawning

Dynamic behavior spawning is a fundamental aspect of marine autonomy, sensing, and communications. It involves the ability of an autonomous marine vehicle to generate new behaviors or actions in response to changes in its environment or mission objectives. This ability is essential for the robustness and adaptability of marine autonomous systems.

#### 11.1b.1 Principles of Basic Dynamic Behavior Spawning

The principles of basic dynamic behavior spawning are based on the concept of hierarchical finite-state machines (HFSMs). Each actor in a system, such as an autonomous marine vehicle, has a behavior defined by a HFSM. This behavior is a directed graph consisting of nodes called "states" and edges called "transitions". State transitions are triggered by incoming messages from an internal or external end port.

During the state change, certain pieces of code are executed. The programmer can attach these codes to the states and transitions. In the case of marine autonomy, these codes are often responsible for generating new behaviors or actions. For example, if an autonomous marine vehicle detects a change in its environment, such as a sudden increase in water current, it may need to spawn a new behavior to adapt to this change.

#### 11.1b.2 Techniques for Basic Dynamic Behavior Spawning

There are several techniques for basic dynamic behavior spawning, each with its own advantages and limitations. One common technique is the use of implicit data structures, which allow for efficient storage and retrieval of data. This can be particularly useful for large-scale marine autonomy systems, where the ability to quickly access and process data is crucial.

Another technique is the use of artificial intuition, which involves the use of machine learning algorithms to generate new behaviors or actions. This approach can be particularly useful for complex and dynamic environments, where traditional rule-based approaches may not be sufficient.

#### 11.1b.3 Applications of Basic Dynamic Behavior Spawning

Basic dynamic behavior spawning has a wide range of applications in marine autonomy. One of the most common applications is in the development of autonomous underwater vehicles (AUVs). AUVs often operate in complex and dynamic environments, and the ability to spawn new behaviors allows them to adapt to these environments and perform their missions effectively.

Another application is in the development of autonomous surface vehicles (ASVs). ASVs often operate in crowded and unpredictable environments, and the ability to spawn new behaviors allows them to navigate these environments safely and efficiently.

In addition, basic dynamic behavior spawning is also used in the development of autonomous marine systems for environmental monitoring and exploration. These systems often operate in remote and unpredictable environments, and the ability to spawn new behaviors allows them to collect data and perform their missions effectively.

#### 11.1b.4 Future Directions

As marine autonomy continues to advance, the need for more sophisticated and adaptable systems will only increase. This will require the development of more advanced techniques for dynamic behavior spawning. One promising direction is the use of artificial intuition, which can learn from experience and generate new behaviors in response to changing environments.

Another direction is the use of multi-agent systems, where multiple autonomous agents work together to achieve a common goal. This can be particularly useful in complex and dynamic environments, where the ability to spawn new behaviors and coordinate with other agents can greatly enhance the performance of the system.

In conclusion, basic dynamic behavior spawning is a crucial aspect of marine autonomy, sensing, and communications. It allows autonomous marine vehicles to adapt to changing environments and perform their missions effectively. As marine autonomy continues to advance, the development of more advanced techniques for dynamic behavior spawning will be essential for the continued success of these systems.

#### 11.1c Advanced Dynamic Behavior Spawning

Advanced dynamic behavior spawning is a more complex form of dynamic behavior spawning that is used in marine autonomy. It involves the use of advanced techniques and algorithms to generate new behaviors or actions in response to changes in the environment or mission objectives. This ability is crucial for the robustness and adaptability of marine autonomous systems.

#### 11.1c.1 Principles of Advanced Dynamic Behavior Spawning

The principles of advanced dynamic behavior spawning are based on the concept of hierarchical finite-state machines (HFSMs). However, in advanced dynamic behavior spawning, the HFSMs are more complex and can include multiple layers of states and transitions. This allows for more sophisticated and nuanced behaviors to be generated.

Similar to basic dynamic behavior spawning, state transitions are triggered by incoming messages from an internal or external end port. However, in advanced dynamic behavior spawning, these messages can be more complex and can include information about the current state of the system, the environment, and the mission objectives.

During the state change, certain pieces of code are executed. These codes can be more complex and can involve the use of machine learning algorithms, artificial intuition, and other advanced techniques. The programmer can attach these codes to the states and transitions, and they are often responsible for generating new behaviors or actions.

#### 11.1c.2 Techniques for Advanced Dynamic Behavior Spawning

There are several techniques for advanced dynamic behavior spawning, each with its own advantages and limitations. One common technique is the use of implicit data structures, which allow for efficient storage and retrieval of data. This can be particularly useful for large-scale marine autonomy systems, where the ability to quickly access and process data is crucial.

Another technique is the use of artificial intuition, which involves the use of machine learning algorithms to generate new behaviors or actions. This approach can be particularly useful for complex and dynamic environments, where traditional rule-based approaches may not be sufficient.

#### 11.1c.3 Applications of Advanced Dynamic Behavior Spawning

Advanced dynamic behavior spawning has a wide range of applications in marine autonomy. One of the most common applications is in the development of autonomous underwater vehicles (AUVs). AUVs often operate in complex and dynamic environments, and the ability to spawn new behaviors allows them to adapt to these environments and perform their missions effectively.

Another application is in the development of autonomous surface vehicles (ASVs). ASVs often operate in crowded and unpredictable environments, and the ability to spawn new behaviors allows them to navigate these environments safely and efficiently.

In addition, advanced dynamic behavior spawning is also used in the development of autonomous marine systems for environmental monitoring and exploration. These systems often operate in remote and unpredictable environments, and the ability to spawn new behaviors allows them to collect data and perform their missions effectively.

#### 11.1c.4 Future Directions

As marine autonomy continues to advance, the need for more sophisticated and adaptable systems will only increase. This will require the development of even more advanced techniques for dynamic behavior spawning. One promising direction is the use of artificial intuition, which can learn from experience and generate new behaviors in response to changing environments.

Another direction is the use of multi-agent systems, where multiple autonomous agents work together to achieve a common goal. This approach can be particularly useful in complex and dynamic environments, where the ability to spawn new behaviors and coordinate with other agents can greatly enhance the performance of the system.




#### 11.1c Advanced Dynamic Behavior Spawning

Advanced dynamic behavior spawning is a more complex form of behavior generation that is often used in marine autonomy systems. It involves the ability of an autonomous marine vehicle to generate new behaviors or actions in response to changes in its environment or mission objectives, but with a higher level of sophistication and adaptability.

#### 11.1c.1 Principles of Advanced Dynamic Behavior Spawning

The principles of advanced dynamic behavior spawning are based on the concept of hierarchical finite-state machines (HFSMs), similar to basic dynamic behavior spawning. However, in advanced dynamic behavior spawning, the HFSMs are often more complex and interconnected, allowing for a greater degree of flexibility and adaptability.

Each actor in a system, such as an autonomous marine vehicle, has a behavior defined by a HFSM. This behavior is a directed graph consisting of nodes called "states" and edges called "transitions". State transitions are triggered by incoming messages from an internal or external end port.

During the state change, certain pieces of code are executed. The programmer can attach these codes to the states and transitions. In the case of marine autonomy, these codes are often responsible for generating new behaviors or actions. For example, if an autonomous marine vehicle detects a change in its environment, such as a sudden increase in water current, it may need to spawn a new behavior to adapt to this change.

#### 11.1c.2 Techniques for Advanced Dynamic Behavior Spawning

There are several techniques for advanced dynamic behavior spawning, each with its own advantages and limitations. One common technique is the use of implicit data structures, which allow for efficient storage and retrieval of data. This can be particularly useful for large-scale marine autonomy systems, where the ability to quickly access and process data is crucial.

Another technique is the use of artificial intuition, which involves the use of machine learning algorithms to generate new behaviors or actions. This approach can be particularly useful for complex and dynamic environments, where traditional rule-based approaches may not be sufficient.

#### 11.1c.3 Advanced Dynamic Behavior Spawning in Marine Autonomy

In marine autonomy, advanced dynamic behavior spawning is crucial for the successful operation of autonomous marine vehicles. These vehicles often operate in complex and dynamic environments, where traditional rule-based approaches may not be sufficient. Advanced dynamic behavior spawning allows these vehicles to adapt to changes in their environment and mission objectives, ensuring their robustness and adaptability.

For example, an autonomous marine vehicle may need to spawn a new behavior to adapt to a sudden increase in water current. This behavior may involve changing the vehicle's speed, direction, or depth to navigate through the current. The vehicle may also need to spawn a new behavior to adapt to changes in its mission objectives, such as a change in the target location or a change in the mission's priority.

In conclusion, advanced dynamic behavior spawning is a crucial aspect of marine autonomy, sensing, and communications. It allows autonomous marine vehicles to adapt to changes in their environment and mission objectives, ensuring their robustness and adaptability. By understanding the principles and techniques of advanced dynamic behavior spawning, we can design more sophisticated and adaptable marine autonomy systems.

These behaviors allow marine vehicles to interact with other vehicles and objects in their environment, enabling them to perform tasks such as collision avoidance, object tracking, and information sharing. This technology has the potential to revolutionize the way marine operations are conducted, making them more efficient, cost-effective, and safer.

Acoustic communications have been widely used in the marine industry due to their ability to operate in the harsh underwater environment. Unlike electromagnetic waves, acoustic waves can penetrate through water and can travel long distances. This makes them ideal for communication between AUVs and surface vessels, especially in deep-sea operations where other communication methods may not be feasible.
